Abstract 1 Introduction 2 Methodology 2.1 Field investigation and problem framing 2.2 Method overview and scenario design 2.3 Model formulation and implementation in PyPSA 2.4 Refrigeration device demand modelling 2.5 Sensitivity analysis of thermal storage parameters 3 Results 3.1 Empirical characterisation of market-scale cooling constraints 3.2 Cost comparison between battery-based and PCM-based cold storage 3.3 Inter-market cold energy flows and system cost outcomes 3.4 Sensitivity of system cost and storage allocation to PCM parameters 4 Discussion 4.1 Substitution between batteries and PCM in markets 4.2 Substitution through mobile cold storage 4.3 Implications for cold-chain design and policy 4.4 Effects on vendors, market dynamics, and stakeholders 4.5 Limitations and future work 5 Conclusion References License: CC BY 4.0 arXiv:2605.05987v1 [physics.soc-ph] 07 May 2026 \cormark [1] \credit Conceptualization, Methodology, Data curation, Software, Visualization, Writing - Original draft preparation, Writing - review&editing \credit Data curation, Writing - review \cormark [2] \credit Conceptualization, Methdology, Supervision, Writing - review&editing 1]organization=Department of Engineering, King's College London, London WC2R 2LS, UK 2]organization=Department of Engineering Science, University of Oxford, Oxford, OX1 3PJ, UK \cortext [cor1]Corresponding author: hange.lao@kcl.ac.uk \cortext [cor2]Principal corresponding author: wei.4.he@kcl.ac.uk \nonumnote The short version of the paper was presented at ICAE2025, Bangkok, Thailand, Dec. 8-12, 2025. This paper is a substantial extension of the short version of the conference paper. Mobile Cold Energy Storage: Coupling Food Distribution and Energy Systems Hange Lao Binjian Nie Wei He [ [ Abstract Reliable cold storage remains a major constraint in informal food systems across sub-Saharan Africa. Perishable foods are mainly traded in open-air markets, where electricity access is often unstable or unavailable. Conventional cold-chain solutions that rely on continuous grid electricity or large battery systems are expensive and difficult to scale in these settings. This study develops a techno-economic framework to examine how cold energy can be generated, stored, and shared within informal food systems. We use open-air meat markets in Nigeria as a case study. The model integrates solar-powered refrigeration, phase change material (PCM) thermal storage, and inter-market food transport. It identifies least-cost combinations of energy supply, storage capacity and cold distribution strategies. A key feature of the model is that pre-chilled meat is treated as a mobile carrier of thermal energy, while PCM storage remains a stationary storage component within each market. This design links decentralised cold storage with existing trade routes and allows cold energy to move between markets through normal food transport. The results show that partial replacement of battery by PCM-based thermal storage can reduce total system cost by up to 15% compared with battery-only systems, primarily by substituting a significant share of electrical storage with stored cold energy and reducing battery capacity requirements. Sensitivity analysis shows that the PCM competitiveness depends on its capital cost, discharge efficiency, and operational flexibility. Allowing cold energy exchange between markets further reduces system investment (by up to 8%) by reallocating refrigeration and storage capacity across locations. Across all scenarios, the cost of cooling meat remains a small fraction of product value. This suggests that the proposed system can provide affordable cooling even under conservative assumptions. Beyond the Nigerian case, the modelling approach can be applied to other infrastructure-constrained food networks, where treating cold as an energy vector may enable more resilient and cost-effective cold-chain design. keywords: solar energy\sep sustainable supply chain \sep thermal energy storage\sep food security\sep micro-grid\sep food-energy nexus {graphicalabstract} {highlights} A techno-economic framework for decentralized cold storage in informal markets Food products modeled as mobile carriers of cold energy in trade networks PCM thermal storage reduces system cost compared with battery storage Inter-market cold exchange lowers storage needs and system investment Coupling food logistics with energy systems improves cold-chain design 1 Introduction Nigeria, with over 200 million people, is the most populous country in Africa. Agriculture forms the backbone of its economy, accounting for over 24% of total gross domestic product (GDP) in 2020 and employing about two-thirds of the population [ onwude2023bottlenecks] . Within this sector, livestock production plays a crucial role in food security and nutrition, with meat providing a significant share of dietary protein in Sub-Saharan Africa (SSA) [ smith2022modeling, roesel2014food] . Across Nigeria and much of SSA, perishable foods are predominantly traded through informal open-air markets. These systems account for more than 80% of food sales and remain central to rural–urban food distribution [ grace2014taking, hannah2022persistence] . However, they operate with limited infrastructure, weak cold-chain capacity, and minimal electricity access [ roesel2014food, adesola2024challenges] . As a result, post-harvest losses are substantial. Nigeria loses approximately 40% of agricultural output annually, with associated income losses and greenhouse gas emissions [ onwude2023bottlenecks, seth2020nigeria] . Informal meat markets are particularly exposed due to inadequate refrigeration, sanitation, and storage infrastructure [ adesokan2021informal] . Cold-chain systems based on vapor-compression refrigeration are effective in reducing spoilage [ amjad2023decentralized, yamauchi2023solar] , but their deployment in informal markets is constrained by high capital costs and unreliable electricity supply [ amjad2023decentralized, xue2014optimal] . Solar-powered refrigeration offers an alternative in regions with high solar potential, including Nigeria [ iea2022africa, shaaban2014renewable] . However, solar intermittency requires storage solutions to ensure continuous cooling. Battery-based storage is commonly used but significantly increases system cost and maintenance requirements [ amini2025life] . Although recent studies demonstrate the technical feasibility of off-grid and hybrid solar cold storage systems [ mehta2025mini, meyer2022design, ayodele2021off] , cost-effective and scalable storage strategies for informal markets remain unresolved. Thermal energy storage using phase change materials (PCM) has been proposed as an alternative approach for stabilising refrigeration systems powered by intermittent renewable energy [ selvnes2021review, bista2018performance] . By enabling cooling to be generated and stored locally without continuous electricity supply, PCM-based systems create distributed points of cold generation. However, existing approaches typically treat cooling infrastructure as a stationary energy system, independent of food trade dynamics [ iyer2025cold] . In informal market networks, food moves continuously between spatially distributed markets. When food is cooled in markets equipped with PCM-based storage, the retained cooling travels with the product through existing trade routes. In this configuration, food acts as a mobile carrier of cold energy, while PCM remains stationary at the point of generation. Despite growing interest in decentralised refrigeration, current studies do not explicitly model cold energy exchange within informal trade networks or quantify its system-level cost implications. To address this gap, this study develops a techno-economic optimisation framework to analyse how cold energy can be generated, stored, and exchanged across informal food systems. Using open-air meat markets (OAMM) in Nigeria as a case study, the framework integrates solar-powered refrigeration, PCM-based storage, and inter-market food transport within a unified optimisation model. By representing cooled food as a mobile carrier of thermal energy, the model captures interactions between decentralised energy infrastructure and trade flows, and determines least-cost configurations of generation, storage, and cold distribution strategies. 2 Methodology This section describes the methodological framework used to evaluate decentralised cooling across OAMM in Nigeria. The approach combines field investigation with techno-economic optimisation modelling. 2.1 Field investigation and problem framing Field investigations were conducted in August 2023 across five major open-air meat markets (OAMMs) in Abuja (Kubwa, Dei Dei, Orange, Kabusa and Lugbe). The selected sites represent variation in market sizes and spatial context. the objective was to characterise market structure, meat distribution patterns, refrigeration practices and electricity access conditions. Data collection included observations and informal interviews with meat vendors, which provided qualitative insights into operational routines, refrigeration methods, and cooperative behaviors among market participants. Particular attention was given to the multifunctional nature of OAMMs. An additional visit to a large open-air fish market (Kado) provided a reference case of shared cold storage powered by collectively operated diesel generators. These observations were used to define system boundaries and inform key modelling assumptions, including representative load profiles, daily operating schedules, cooling reliability requirements, and the feasibility of shared infrastructure at the market scale. Quantitative parameters (e.g., cooling demand, technology performance, and cost inputs) were derived from load simulations and published literature. 2.2 Method overview and scenario design Based on the system characterisation, an hourly techno-economic optimisation model (Figure 1) was developed. The model determines least-cost configurations of solar photovoltaic (PV) generation, refrigeration capacity, and energy storage for decentralised cold-chain systems. Two storage technologies are considered: battery storage and phase change material (PCM)–based thermal storage integrated with refrigeration units. Three scenarios are evaluated: • S1 (Battery-based decentralised systems): Each market operates an independent solar PV system with battery storage to ensure continuous cooling. • S2 (PCM-based decentralised systems): PCM-based thermal storage is introduced at each market to substitute a large share of battery storage. PCM provides thermal buffering and reduces the system's reliance on electrical storage, while batteries are retained for short-term balancing. • S3 (PCM with inter-market cold exchange): Building on S2, pre-chilled meat can be transported between markets. In this configuration, retained cooling embodied in the meat mass is modelled as a mobile thermal energy carrier. Cold-energy transfers between markets are represented as endogenous decision variables and optimised jointly with generation, storage, and refrigeration capacities. Across all scenarios, system performance is evaluated using annualised total system cost as the primary metric. Cost components associated with generation, storage, and refrigeration are analysed separately. Sensitivity analysis is conducted on key cost and demand parameters to assess result robustness. Figure 1: Overview of the modelling approach.(a) Field observations and stakeholder engagement used to define system boundaries and modelling assumptions; (b) key conceptual implications derived from field insights that motivate the representation of storage technologies and inter-market interactions; (c) the energy system model and scenario design, in which three configurations (S1–S3) are optimised using PyPSA. 2.3 Model formulation and implementation in PyPSA An hourly linear techno-economic optimisation model was formulated and implemented using the open-source Python for Power System Analysis (PyPSA) framework [ PyPSA] . The model represents decentralised cold storage systems at the market scale, where each OAMM is modelled as a node comprising two energy carriers: electricity and cold (Figure 2). The electricity bus includes solar PV generation and, where applicable, battery storage. Electricity is converted into cooling via a refrigeration unit represented as a link with a fixed coefficient of performance (COP). Cooling demand is defined on the cold bus using simulated hourly load profiles described in the following section. PCM storage is connected to the cold bus and enables thermal energy to be stored and discharged independently of real-time electricity supply. Additional details of the optimisation formulation are provided in the SI Note 1. The optimisation is performed at hourly resolution over the full year 2019. Solar resource data for Abuja (9.0643°N, 7.4893°E) are obtained from Renewables.ninja [ pfenninger2016long] . Given the close spatial proximity of the five markets, identical solar availability profiles are assumed. PV generation is represented using normalised hourly availability factors implemented through the PyPSA parameter p  _  m  a  x  _  p  u p_max_pu and each market operates an independent PV installation. The model is formulated as a linear programming problem that minimises annualised total system cost, including investment in PV capacity, refrigeration units, battery storage (in S1), and PCM storage (in S2–S3). Investment decisions and hourly operational variables are optimised simultaneously. The optimisation is subject to electricity and cold energy balance constraints, storage state-of-charge dynamics, installed capacity limits, and full satisfaction of hourly cooling demand. Capital expenditures are annualised using standard annuity formulations based on technology lifetimes and a specified discount rate. Fixed and variable operation and maintenance costs are excluded due to their relatively small contribution to total cost in small-scale off-grid cold storage systems. The three scenarios differ in storage configuration and network topology. In S1, each market operates a decentralised PV system with battery storage connected to the electricity bus. In S2, PCM-based thermal storage is connected to cold bus for partially substituting battery storage, reducing the need for electrical storage. In S3, inter-market links are introduced between cold buses to represent the transport of pre-chilled meat and the associated relocation of retained cooling. In this configuration, cooled meat is modelled as a mobile carrier of thermal energy, while PCM remains stationary at the point of cooling. Cold-energy transfers between markets are treated as endogenous decision variables and optimised jointly with generation, storage, and refrigeration capacities. Inter-market links are assumed to be lossless and unconstrained in capacity, representing an upper-bound estimate of the potential benefits of cold-energy exchange through existing trade networks. Transport costs are represented as marginal costs per unit of transferred cold energy. No additional electricity consumption for transport is modelled. Model outputs include least-cost system configurations, installed capacities of generation and storage technologies, hourly electricity and cold energy balances, and the spatial distribution of cold-energy flows in Scenario S3. Figure 2: Outline of the cold storage energy system and the capital costs considered for each components (PV panels [ takeshima2023solar] , battery storage [ brown2018synergies] , PCM storage [ tong2021phase] and refrigeration devices [ razmi2020thermoeconomic] ) 2.4 Refrigeration device demand modelling Hourly refrigeration demand at each market was estimated using a first-principles thermal model based on sensible heat removal (Eq. 1). The total daily cooling energy required to reduce meat temperature from an initial temperature ( T i  n  i  t  i  a  l = 40 ∘  C T_{initial}=40~^{\circ}C ) to the target storage temperature ( T t  a  r  g  e  t = 4 ∘  C T_{target}=4~^{\circ}C ) E m  e  a  t = m  c p  Δ  T E_{meat}=mc_{p}\Delta T (1) where E m  e  a  t  ( t ) E_{meat}(t) is the total daily sensible cooling energy associated with the meat throughput, m m the daily meat mass, c p c_{p} the specific heat capacity of beef (3.14 kJ/kgK is used). The initial temperature of 40 °C represents an upper-bound ambient condition for freshly handled meat in open-air markets. Daily meat quantities were derived from vendor interviews across five OAMMs in Abuja. Animal counts were converted to meat mass using an average carcass yield of 275 kg per cattle [ vasko2022opportunities] . The resulting daily cooling energy was distributed across 24 hours to generate time-resolved refrigeration demand profiles. Two operating regimes were defined: a daytime regime (04:00–19:00) representing slaughtering and active trading hours with elevated cooling demand, and a night-time regime (19:00–04:00) representing residual refrigeration of unsold meat. Within each regime, hourly load fractions were generated as non-negative and normalised values that preserve total daily cooling energy while introducing intra-day variability. The generated cooling demand profile is provided in SI Note 2. The modelling approach assumes uniform product temperature during cooling, constant specific heat capacity, negligible microbial heat generation, and no additional external heat gains during the initial cooling process. These assumptions isolate the dominant first-order thermal process governing refrigeration demand in open-air market conditions. 2.5 Sensitivity analysis of thermal storage parameters A sensitivity analysis was conducted to evaluate the influence of PCM storage parameters on system cost and technology allocation. Two parameters were varied: PCM capital cost and discharge efficiency. PCM capital cost was varied from $0.75 to $168.10 per kWh in $1 increments, covering reported cost ranges and extending to the cost level of battery storage. Discharge efficiency, defined as the fraction of stored cold energy recoverable to meet demand, was varied from 0.1 to 1.0 in increments of 0.05. For each parameter combination, the optimisation model was solved for all five markets while holding all other inputs constant. No structural changes were introduced relative to the baseline formulation. The optimisation endogenously determines the least-cost allocation among PCM storage, battery storage, and refrigeration capacity. Outputs recorded for each run include total annual system cost and installed capacities of PCM and battery storage. Refrigeration capacity and runtime are additionally extracted to assess operational impacts associated with PCM charging requirements and changes in storage technology mix. 3 Results 3.1 Empirical characterisation of market-scale cooling constraints Field investigations conducted across five open-air meat markets (OAMMs) in Abuja provide market-level operational data that are not captured in national statistics or publicly available datasets (the observation summary and photos are in SI Note 3 and Figure S2-6). In particular, quantitative information on daily meat throughput, operating hours, refrigeration practices, electricity access conditions, and inter-market redistribution patterns is largely absent from formal records. These empirical findings define key boundary conditions for decentralised cold-chain modelling. Figure 3: Five OAMMs visited in Abuja and the respective scale (* this value is estimated by the authors based on interviews with meat vendors) The five markets differ in daily cattle throughput and spatial location within the city (Figure 3), but exhibit consistent structural characteristics. All sites function as multifunctional nodes integrating livestock holding, slaughtering, butchering, retail, and short-distance redistribution within a single physical space (Figure 4). Meat products are frequently transported between markets during trading hours, indicating that markets operate as interconnected nodes within a distributed trade network rather than as isolated retail endpoints. Figure 4: Multifunctional OAMM that includes keeping the animal, slaughtering, selling and redistribution Electricity access across all observed meat markets is intermittent and unreliable. Continuous mechanical refrigeration was not observed. Vendors primarily rely on ice blocks during daytime trading to slow spoilage under high ambient temperatures. In contrast, a large open-air fish market operated a shared cold room powered by a diesel generator, requiring collective fuel purchase and maintenance. Comparable fuel-based refrigeration systems were not present in meat markets, suggesting financial and operational barriers to continuous cooling technologies. Cooperative practices related to energy use were observed at multiple sites. Traders routinely pool financial resources to purchase fuel or maintain shared equipment, demonstrating the existence of informal collective management mechanisms. Combined with frequent inter-market product redistribution, these findings indicate that cold-chain interventions must account for both decentralised operation at the market level and network-level interaction across markets. Together, these empirical results establish three system-level constraints for cold-chain design in Nigeria's informal meat sector: (i) absence of continuous grid-based refrigeration, (ii) limited access to capital-intensive storage technologies, and (iii) existing spatial linkages between markets through routine product redistribution. These constraints motivate the decentralised and network-integrated modelling framework evaluated in the subsequent analysis. 3.2 Cost comparison between battery-based and PCM-based cold storage Across the five markets, integrating PCM-based thermal storage (S2) reduces total annualised system cost up to 15%, with an average reduction of 11%. relative to the battery-only configuration (S1). This cost reduction is primarily driven by substantial decreases in required battery capacity. Figure 5 shows the optimised capacities under both scenarios. When PCM storage is introduced, installed battery capacity decreases by approximately 67% on average across markets. PV capacity increases modestly (around 4%) to accommodate daytime charging of thermal storage. Installed refrigeration capacity is reduced by roughly 50%, reflecting a shift from continuous high-power cooling toward load shifting through stored cold energy. PCM substitutes for a substantial share of electrical storage while batteries remain necessary for balancing. Although S2 requires additional investment in thermal storage and slightly larger PV arrays, these costs are more than offset by the reduction in battery capacity, resulting in lower total system cost. Figure 5: Capacity and cost comparison between S1 and S2 Hourly operation patterns illustrate the underlying mechanism (Figure 6). In S1, early morning and evening cooling demand is supplied primarily through battery discharge due to the temporal mismatch between solar generation and refrigeration demand. This results in deep daily battery cycling. In S2, part of the daytime solar output is diverted to charge PCM storage, which is later discharged to meet evening cooling demand. Battery operation is consequently reduced and limited mainly to short-term balancing. The system thus relies more on stored thermal energy and less on electrical storage to manage solar intermittency. (a) Electricity storage scenario (S1) (b) Thermal storage scenario (S2) Figure 6: Cold demand and supply alignment comparison 3.3 Inter-market cold energy flows and system cost outcomes Allowing inter-market cold energy exchange (S3) reduces total system cost by 8% relative to the isolated PCM-based configuration (S2) (Table 1). The cost reduction is primarily driven by a 35% decrease in required PCM storage capacity at the system level, while total PV capacity remains nearly unchanged. Table 1: Relative changes (%) in system cost and capacity decisions when enabling inter-market cold energy transfer via chilled meat, compared with S2 (negative values indicate reductions) The introduction of cold-energy transfer enables spatial reallocation of cooling resources across markets. Rather than sizing PCM storage independently at each site to meet local peak demand, the optimisation exploits cross-market exchange to balance temporal mismatches collectively. As a result, aggregate storage requirements decline without requiring additional generation capacity. At the individual market level, cost impacts are heterogeneous. Kabusa and Orange experience the largest cost reductions (4% each), accompanied by decreases in both PV and PCM capacities. Lugbe and Dei Dei show modest cost reductions with relatively small capacity adjustments. In contrast, Kubwa exhibits a slight cost increase (+3%), associated with higher PV and refrigeration capacities despite reduced PCM storage. This indicates that Kubwa functions primarily as a net exporter of cold energy within the optimised network, investing in additional generation and refrigeration capacity to supply other markets. Export and import shares increase across all markets once transfer is enabled, confirming active utilisation of inter-market cold flows. These results demonstrate that cold exchange allows decentralised systems to operate as a coordinated network, reducing aggregate storage requirements through spatial diversification of cooling demand. 3.4 Sensitivity of system cost and storage allocation to PCM parameters Sensitivity analysis is performed on the decentralised PCM-based configuration (S2) to isolate storage substitution dynamics without confounding effects from inter-market cold exchange. By holding network topology constant, the analysis characterises how PCM capital cost and discharge efficiency jointly determine storage allocation under decentralised operation. Across all five markets, PCM competitiveness depends on both its relative capital cost and discharge efficiency. A clear transition frontier separates PCM-dominated solutions from battery-dominated solutions in the cost–efficiency parameter space (Figure 7). PCM is selected when it is sufficiently inexpensive and exhibits moderate-to-high discharge efficiency, whereas battery storage dominates as the PCM-to-battery cost ratio increases or discharge efficiency declines. The position of this frontier varies across markets. Lugbe, the largest market, exhibits the broadest PCM-preferred region, indicating that higher cooling demand improves the economic viability of thermal storage. Orange and Dei Dei display intermediate behaviour, while Kabusa and Kubwa show narrower PCM-preferred regions, with PCM selected only under favourable cost and efficiency conditions. These results suggest that market scale influences storage substitution patterns, with larger markets better able to exploit thermal storage economies. Figure 7: Effect of PCM efficiency and price on the choice between PCM and battery storage. To contextualise the model-derived frontier, reported properties of two representative PCM materials commonly used in refrigeration applications are summarised in Table 2. The selected materials, n-tetradecane (organic PCM) and sodium sulfate decahydrate (inorganic PCM), are used as illustrative benchmarks spanning typical ranges of latent heat, density, discharge efficiency, and capital cost reported in the literature. Figure 8 positions these materials within the cost–efficiency parameter space. Both materials occupy a discharge efficiency range of 0.7–0.9 and correspond to PCM-to-battery cost ratios of approximately 0.11–0.23. Within this range, system cost increases markedly as the cost ratio rises, indicating that relative capital cost exerts a stronger influence on competitiveness than variations within the considered efficiency band. Sodium sulfate decahydrate, positioned at lower cost ratios (0.11–0.13), lies closer to the minimum-cost envelope than n-tetradecane (0.17–0.23), suggesting greater economic viability under the assumed cost parameters. Table 2: Properties and cost parameters of selected PCMs [ tong2021phase] Figure 8: Benchmarking real PCM candidates against the model-derived minimum-cost envelope in Lugbe. The solid curve shows the minimum system cost across the PCM cost–efficiency parameter sweep, while the dashed line indicates the battery-only benchmark. Markers represent reported cost and discharge efficiency ranges for sodium sulfate decahydrate and n-tetradecane (C14). Refrigeration charging time further shapes storage allocation (Figure 9). Total system cost decreases as the available charging window increases. Short charging durations (below approximately 3–4 hours) correspond to battery-dominated solutions, as meeting daily cooling demand within a narrow time window requires high instantaneous charging power. In these cases, batteries provide short-timescale flexibility without necessitating oversized refrigeration capacity. As charging time increases, peak power requirements decline, enabling a larger share of storage to be provided by PCM, reflected in rising PCM-to-battery capacity ratios and lower overall system cost. Figure 9: Effect of refrigeration-device runtime and PCM capacity on total system cost. 4 Discussion This study identifies two linked substitution mechanisms in decentralised cold chains. First, PCM substitutes for part of the battery function by shifting cooling across time. Second, once chilled food moves between markets, part of the storage function itself becomes mobile, allowing cold generated in one market to serve demand in another. In the Abuja case, the PCM-based configuration reduces annualised system cost by up to 15%, with an average reduction of 11%, mainly through a roughly 67% reduction in battery capacity. Allowing inter-market cold exchange reduces total system cost by a further 8% and aggregate PCM capacity by 35% relative to the isolated PCM case. The main contribution is therefore not only a comparison of storage technologies, but a demonstration that cold-chain design in informal markets depends on how storage is allocated across both time and space. 4.1 Substitution between batteries and PCM in markets The model shows that PCM becomes favourable under three conditions: low relative capital cost, moderate to high discharge efficiency, and sufficient charging time. In the sensitivity analysis, a clear frontier separates PCM-dominated from battery-dominated solutions. PCM is selected when it is inexpensive enough relative to batteries and can recover a large enough fraction of stored cold; battery storage becomes dominant as PCM cost rises or discharge efficiency falls. The results also show that market scale matters. Lugbe, the largest market, has the broadest PCM-preferred region, while smaller markets such as Kabusa and Kubwa only favour PCM under more restricted cost and efficiency conditions. Charging duration is equally important. When refrigeration must charge within a short window, around 3 to 4 hours or less, the model shifts toward battery-dominated solutions because batteries provide the high instantaneous flexibility needed to meet demand without oversizing refrigeration. As the charging window lengthens, PCM takes a larger role and total system cost falls. The mechanism is therefore specific: PCM is most valuable when daily cooling demand is predictable and can be pre-produced over a long enough daytime window, while batteries remain valuable when flexibility must be delivered quickly and under tighter operating schedules. 4.2 Substitution through mobile cold storage The inter-market mechanism only works when three conditions are present: routine product redistribution between nearby markets, unequal local cooling needs across the network, and at least some markets that can economically export retained cold. Under these conditions, markets no longer need to size PCM independently against local peaks. Instead, part of the storage requirement is shifted into trade flows, which is why allowing cold exchange cuts aggregate PCM capacity by 35% while leaving total PV capacity almost unchanged. This shows that the main benefit of mobile cold exchange is not more generation, but better spatial allocation of storage and refrigeration resources. The benefits are also uneven. Kabusa and Orange see the largest cost reductions, around 4%, while Lugbe and Dei Dei gain only modestly. Kubwa, by contrast, experiences a slight cost increase of 3% because it takes on a net exporter role and invests in more PV and refrigeration capacity to support the network. This suggests that mobile cold storage is most valuable in clustered local market systems with existing short-distance redistribution and asymmetric market roles, especially where some nodes can act as cooling hubs rather than every market being built as a fully independent system. 4.3 Implications for cold-chain design and policy These results support a shift from isolated-market planning to cluster-based cold-chain design. The model does not suggest removing batteries altogether. Rather, it indicates a more differentiated design logic: PCM can provide the main low-cost storage function where cooling demand is regular and charging windows are long, while batteries remain useful for short-timescale balancing and constrained operating schedules. For policy, this means off-grid cold storage programmes should not default to battery-led designs when the service being provided is predictable cooling rather than general electricity flexibility. The results also suggest that investment should target groups of connected markets rather than single sites in isolation. Because the gains in the inter-market case come mainly from reallocating storage rather than expanding PV, effective policy will depend as much on route structure, handling rules, and temperature governance as on the hardware itself. In the Nigerian and wider African context, where informal food systems already rely on short-distance redistribution and shared practices, these networks should be treated not only as infrastructure-poor systems but also as coordination structures that can support more distributed forms of cold-chain development. 4.4 Effects on vendors, market dynamics, and stakeholders The two mechanisms identified here imply different changes for vendors and market actors. The temporal substitution mechanism mainly reduces dependence on battery investment, which lowers the capital burden of decentralised cooling and may make shared cold storage more financially accessible to trader groups or market associations. The spatial substitution mechanism has a more uneven effect. Because some markets can become net exporters of cold while others mainly import it, the benefits of the network are not distributed equally. In this study, Kubwa takes on extra investment to support exchange, while markets such as Kabusa and Orange gain larger cost reductions. In practice, this could reshape bargaining relationships between hub markets, smaller dependent markets, transporters, and storage operators. Vendors may benefit from lower spoilage risk, more flexible selling times, and better inventory control, but they may also become more dependent on whoever controls shared cooling assets or access to exported cold. Since field observations already show both inter-market redistribution and collective resource-sharing practices, the institutional question is not whether coordination is possible, but how costs, access, and decision-making are organised. Once cold storage becomes partly mobile, the system is no longer only a technical infrastructure problem; it also becomes a question of governance, pricing, and trust across the market network. 4.5 Limitations and future work Several limits should qualify the current findings. The inter-market case is best understood as an upper-bound estimate because transfers are modelled in simplified form, without explicit transport losses, route constraints, or vehicle energy use. Cooling demand is also simplified around sensible heat removal for a single product type and does not explicitly capture detailed quality decay, microbial processes, or broader food heterogeneity. In addition, the empirical base is limited to a small Abuja market network, and the model does not endogenise financing, ownership, maintenance capacity, or stakeholder behaviour. These limits do not remove the core insight, but they do mean that the quantitative savings should be interpreted as structural potential rather than deployment-ready outcomes. Future work should therefore extend the framework in four directions. First, it should include multiple food types as distinct thermal vectors, since meat, fish, dairy, and produce differ in heat capacity, spoilage dynamics, and transport value. Second, it should test the concept at larger regional scales with longer routes, more diverse climates, and more complex market hierarchies. Third, it should integrate explicit transport losses, travel times, and time-temperature quality dynamics. Fourth, it should examine ownership models, pricing rules, and cooperative governance through field-based pilots. More broadly, the concept developed here opens a wider research agenda on material-embedded energy storage in infrastructure-constrained systems, where useful energy may travel not only through wires and fuels, but also through goods moving within economic networks. 5 Conclusion This study develops a techno-economic optimisation framework to evaluate decentralised solar-powered cold storage systems in Nigeria's informal meat markets. By integrating field observations, refrigeration demand modelling, and energy system optimisation, the framework analyses how solar generation, storage technologies, and food logistics jointly determine least-cost cold-chain configurations. The results reveal three key findings. First, thermal storage using PCM can substitute for a significant share of electrical storage by shifting cooling provision across time, reducing battery capacity requirements and lowering system cost under favourable cost conditions. Second, operational flexibility—represented by allowable refrigeration charging duration—strongly influences storage composition, with longer charging windows favouring PCM-based storage and shorter windows favouring battery-based solutions. Third, enabling inter-market cold exchange introduces spatial coordination into decentralised cold systems. Embodied cold energy transported through food distribution networks reduces aggregate PCM capacity requirements and lowers total system cost, demonstrating that mobile cold flows can function as distributed thermal storage. More broadly, the findings highlight how decentralised food distribution networks can be integrated with renewable energy systems through thermal storage and embodied energy flows. By representing chilled products as mobile carriers of cold energy, the framework extends conventional energy system modelling beyond infrastructure-bound energy carriers and provides a basis for analysing coordinated cold-chain design in infrastructure-constrained food systems. \printcredits References Experimental support, please view the build logs for errors. NetFlow & sFlow Monitoring for AI Infrastructure Products Services Resources Pricing Company Contact us Products Services Resources Pricing Company Contact us Get Demo Free Trial Get Demo Free Trial Home About Blog Other Pages Contact us SolarWinds NTA Alternative ManageEngine NetFlow Analyzer Alternative Steadfast DQE Communications OFFSITE FOCUS Broadband Everstream T-2 WhatsUp Gold NTA Alternative LogicMonitor Alternative NetFlow Analyzer Comparisons CacheFly ATE WebNX TierPoint Bandwidth.com Texas Education Telecommunications Network (TETN) Home About Blog Other Pages Contact us SolarWinds NTA Alternative ManageEngine NetFlow Analyzer Alternative Steadfast DQE Communications OFFSITE FOCUS Broadband Everstream T-2 WhatsUp Gold NTA Alternative LogicMonitor Alternative NetFlow Analyzer Comparisons CacheFly ATE WebNX TierPoint Bandwidth.com Texas Education Telecommunications Network (TETN) NetFlow/sFlow Monitoring in AI Environments Noction $ Posts $ Blog $ NetFlow/sFlow Monitoring in AI Environments Mar 12, 2026 If you've spent time supporting AI infrastructure, whether that's a GPU training cluster, a fleet of inference nodes, or a multi-tenant model serving platform, you've probably noticed something: the network telemetry tools that served you well in a traditional data center feel slightly out of place here. Not useless. Just not quite designed for this. The traffic patterns are different. The failure modes are different. The things you need to catch early are different. And if you're running NetFlow or sFlow collection – which you should be – understanding where that data genuinely helps versus where you're looking at the wrong instrument is the difference between a useful monitoring stack and a false sense of coverage. Why AI Traffic Is Different Most of the networking intuition you've built over a career was forged on north-south traffic – clients reaching services, users reaching the internet, workloads reaching storage. Even in modern microservices environments with heavy east-west traffic, flows are relatively short-lived, heterogeneous in size, and largely TCP-based with normal congestion dynamics. AI training breaks most of those assumptions simultaneously. A distributed training job across a GPU cluster is synchronous in a way that most networked workloads are not. Every GPU in a collective operation, say, an AllReduce synchronizing gradients across 512 nodes, must complete its contribution before any node can advance to the next compute step. The network becomes the synchronization barrier. This creates genuinely unusual traffic characteristics: Most flows are elephant flows A gradient synchronization pass over a large cluster moves gigabytes per node in a coordinated burst, not a trickle of small transactions. Whether your flow collector sees this depends on your fabric. If your cluster runs collective communication over TCP/IP – common in cloud-based training environments or clusters without dedicated RDMA hardware – you will see sustained multi-gigabit flows where traditional workloads generate thousands of small ones. If your cluster runs RoCEv2 or InfiniBand, this traffic bypasses the IP stack and is largely invisible to NetFlow and sFlow collection. Know your fabric before assuming flow data covers your compute tier. Bursts are synchronized across the whole cluster When AllReduce begins, all nodes transmit simultaneously. This creates incast conditions at aggregation switches – a convergence of many flows to a small number of destinations at the same moment. This is a known and serious problem in AI fabric design. If your collective communication runs over TCP/IP, this shows up in flow data as correlated burst events across many source IPs simultaneously. On RDMA fabrics, the incast problem is equally real and equally damaging to training throughput, but it is not visible to a flow analyzer. Conversation patterns are structured and repetitive GPU-to-GPU communication during AllReduce follows strict topological patterns. In a ring AllReduce each node communicates only with its two neighbors; in a tree AllReduce communication follows a fixed hierarchy. These patterns repeat with every training step. On TCP/IP-based training fabrics this structure is visible in flow data: a fixed set of source-destination IP pairs exchanging large volumes at regular intervals, which makes baseline deviation relatively easy to detect. On RDMA fabrics, this structure exists at the hardware layer but does not surface in flow records. The traffic alternates between distinct phases Training jobs cycle between compute phases and I/O phases. The compute phase is dominated by GPU-to-GPU gradient exchange, typically running over RDMA (RoCEv2 or InfiniBand) – traffic that is largely invisible to NetFlow and sFlow collection. The I/O phase – dataset loading from distributed storage, checkpoint writes – uses different source/destination IP ranges, different timing, and potentially different protocols. Whether I/O phase traffic appears as TCP in your flow data depends on your storage backend: NFS, iSCSI, and S3-compatible object storage all use TCP; RDMA-based parallel filesystems such as Lustre with RDMA, GPFS, DAOS, or NVMe-oF do not. Know which you're running before assuming flow data covers your storage tier. Where Flow Data Actually Earns Its Keep Spotting the Slow Node This is the use case that converts skeptics. Distributed training is only as fast as its slowest participant – the straggler problem is real and expensive. When a training job is consistently underperforming its expected throughput, the first instinct is to look at compute metrics: GPU utilization, memory bandwidth, CUDA errors. Those are valid. But the problem is often in the network, and it shows up first in flow data. If you've defined your GPU nodes as a custom group in NFA and you're watching per-node flow volumes during a training run, a node that's sending substantially less data than its peers stands out immediately. Not because NFA tells you the GPU is underperforming – it can't do that – but because the network traffic is the observable symptom of a node not pulling its weight in the collective operation. If you've spent time supporting AI infrastructure, whether that's a GPU training cluster, a fleet of inference nodes, or a multi-tenant model serving platform, you've probably noticed something: the network telemetry tools that served you well in a traditional data center feel slightly out of place here. Not useless. Just not quite designed for this. The traffic patterns are different. The failure modes are different. The things you need to catch early are different. And if you're running NetFlow or sFlow collection – which you should be – understanding where that data genuinely helps versus where you're looking at the wrong instrument is the difference between a useful monitoring stack and a false sense of coverage. Why AI Traffic Is Different Most of the networking intuition you've built over a career was forged on north-south traffic – clients reaching services, users reaching the internet, workloads reaching storage. Even in modern microservices environments with heavy east-west traffic, flows are relatively short-lived, heterogeneous in size, and largely TCP-based with normal congestion dynamics. AI training breaks most of those assumptions simultaneously. A distributed training job across a GPU cluster is synchronous in a way that most networked workloads are not. Every GPU in a collective operation, say, an AllReduce synchronizing gradients across 512 nodes, must complete its contribution before any node can advance to the next compute step. The network becomes the synchronization barrier. This creates genuinely unusual traffic characteristics: Most flows are elephant flows A gradient synchronization pass over a large cluster moves gigabytes per node in a coordinated burst, not a trickle of small transactions. Whether your flow collector sees this depends on your fabric. If your cluster runs collective communication over TCP/IP – common in cloud-based training environments or clusters without dedicated RDMA hardware – you will see sustained multi-gigabit flows where traditional workloads generate thousands of small ones. If your cluster runs RoCEv2 or InfiniBand, this traffic bypasses the IP stack and is largely invisible to NetFlow and sFlow collection. Know your fabric before assuming flow data covers your compute tier. Bursts are synchronized across the whole cluster When AllReduce begins, all nodes transmit simultaneously. This creates incast conditions at aggregation switches – a convergence of many flows to a small number of destinations at the same moment. This is a known and serious problem in AI fabric design. If your collective communication runs over TCP/IP, this shows up in flow data as correlated burst events across many source IPs simultaneously. On RDMA fabrics, the incast problem is equally real and equally damaging to training throughput, but it is not visible to a flow analyzer. Conversation patterns are structured and repetitive GPU-to-GPU communication during AllReduce follows strict topological patterns. In a ring AllReduce each node communicates only with its two neighbors; in a tree AllReduce communication follows a fixed hierarchy. These patterns repeat with every training step. On TCP/IP-based training fabrics this structure is visible in flow data: a fixed set of source-destination IP pairs exchanging large volumes at regular intervals, which makes baseline deviation relatively easy to detect. On RDMA fabrics, this structure exists at the hardware layer but does not surface in flow records. The traffic alternates between distinct phases Training jobs cycle between compute phases and I/O phases. The compute phase is dominated by GPU-to-GPU gradient exchange, typically running over RDMA (RoCEv2 or InfiniBand) – traffic that is largely invisible to NetFlow and sFlow collection. The I/O phase – dataset loading from distributed storage, checkpoint writes – uses different source/destination IP ranges, different timing, and potentially different protocols. Whether I/O phase traffic appears as TCP in your flow data depends on your storage backend: NFS, iSCSI, and S3-compatible object storage all use TCP; RDMA-based parallel filesystems such as Lustre with RDMA, GPFS, DAOS, or NVMe-oF do not. Know which you're running before assuming flow data covers your storage tier. Where Flow Data Actually Earns Its Keep Spotting the Slow Node This is the use case that converts skeptics. Distributed training is only as fast as its slowest participant – the straggler problem is real and expensive. When a training job is consistently underperforming its expected throughput, the first instinct is to look at compute metrics: GPU utilization, memory bandwidth, CUDA errors. Those are valid. But the problem is often in the network, and it shows up first in flow data. If you've defined your GPU nodes as a custom group in NFA and you're watching per-node flow volumes during a training run, a node that's sending substantially less data than its peers stands out immediately. Not because NFA tells you the GPU is underperforming – it can't do that – but because the network traffic is the observable symptom of a node not pulling its weight in the collective operation. SCOPE BOUNDARY What flow data shows you here: byte and packet counts per source IP, per time window. What it does not show: why the node is slow – driver issue, hardware fault, misconfigured NCCL, bad cable at the NIC. Flow data gets you to the right machine. The diagnosis happens elsewhere. Catching Checkpoint and Dataset I/O Problems The I/O phases of a training job generate traffic that is completely distinct from the GPU-to-GPU communication – different source and destination IP ranges, different timing, and on TCP-based storage backends, a different protocol. If your storage infrastructure uses NFS, iSCSI, or S3-compatible object storage, checkpoint writes and dataset reads will appear as TCP flows in NFA and give you a useful visibility window into whether I/O is occurring at the expected frequency and volume. If your storage fabric uses RDMA-based transport: Lustre with RDMA, GPFS, DAOS, or NVMe-oF, those flows will not appear meaningfully in NetFlow or sFlow records, for the same reason that intra-cluster GPU traffic is largely invisible. Verify your storage protocol before treating the absence of storage traffic in NFA as a problem, or its presence as complete coverage. For TCP-backed storage, flow data can tell you whether checkpoint writes are happening at expected intervals, whether dataset loading is completing before the next compute phase begins, and whether storage traffic is bleeding onto the compute fabric – a misconfiguration that creates unnecessary contention. Those are real findings, available from flow collection with no additional instrumentation, provided the transport assumption holds. Validating That Your Segmentation Actually Works AI infrastructure is almost always built with logical segmentation: training nodes in one VLAN or subnet, management traffic on another, storage traffic on a third. The intent is to isolate management access, prevent job interference, and control what communicates with what. The gap between intended segmentation and actual segmentation is often embarrassing. Flow data closes that gap. Using NFA's custom group feature to define your infrastructure tiers and then querying for inter-group conversations gives you a ground-truth view of what's actually communicating, independent of what your firewall rules say they're supposed to do. A training container that's been misconfigured and is opening connections outside its expected scope will show up in that query. A shadow GPU node that someone spun up and forgot to register will appear as an unexpected source IP. A dependency on an external model hub that nobody documented will show up as a recurring outbound flow to a CDN IP range. None of this requires deep packet inspection. It's all visible in flow metadata. The Security Case: Models Are Worth Stealing A fine-tuned LLM trained on proprietary data, with a carefully curated instruction set and RLHF pass, represents a significant capital investment. It also sits on a storage server somewhere with an IP address that's reachable from inside your network. Flow-based monitoring is not a DLP solution, but it is a very good early warning system for bulk exfiltration, because exfiltration at scale requires moving large volumes of data, and volume is exactly what NetFlow measures. The alert configuration for this in NFA is straightforward: define your model storage servers as a custom group, define your approved external destinations as another group – cloud backup endpoints, specific CDN ranges, known partner IPs, and alert on any significant outbound volume from the model storage group to destinations not in the approved list. Add a time-of-day rule that flags large transfers outside business hours. That covers the most common exfiltration patterns with a few minutes of configuration. What you get when an alert fires: source IP, destination IP, protocol, bytes transferred, duration. What you don't get: what the data was. That determination requires host-level logging or a packet capture. Flow data is the trigger. Investigation uses other tools. What to Actually Configure in NFA for AI Infrastructure Here's what alert rules and custom groups make sense specifically for GPU cluster environments. Custom Groups to Define First Everything useful in NFA for AI environments flows from having your infrastructure logically grouped. Define these before building any alert rules or dashboard queries: Alert Rules Worth Configuring Alert Rules Worth Configuring A Representative Data Explorer Query Although not a common scenario for the straggler detection use case, here's the query logic that can be used in NFA's Data Explorer during or after a training run: What you're looking for: a roughly uniform distribution of sent bytes across all training node IPs. Sort ascending to surface low senders at the top. Any node sitting significantly below the cluster mean across the full training window is your candidate straggler. For checkpoint write visibility, applicable only if your storage backend uses TCP transport, the same structure applies with Source IP in [GPU Training Nodes] and Destination IP in [Storage / Checkpointing], grouped by time interval. You're looking for periodic traffic that matches your checkpoint schedule. If your framework checkpoints on elapsed time or loss improvement rather than a fixed step interval, the pattern will not be regular, and the absence of regularity is not itself a signal of a problem. The bottom line Flow analysis has been a standard network operations tool for decades. In AI environments, it can still be useful, but mainly for the parts of the infrastructure that run over normal TCP/IP networks. Flow data shows traffic volume, direction, timing, and communication patterns; it won't tell you anything about GPU performance or diagnose RDMA fabric issues. Where it tends to help most is with things like spotting large outbound transfers, validating network segmentation, observing dataset loading or checkpoint traffic on TCP-based storage, and supporting capacity planning with real traffic data. Tools like Noction Flow Analyzer work well for this kind of visibility because they let you group infrastructure components, explore traffic patterns, and create versatile alerts. If you already collect flow data from your GPU infrastructure, organizing it around your compute, storage, and external networks can make it easier to notice when traffic patterns start to drift from the norm. Network Flow Monitoring, also known as packet sampling, aims… Robert Grimmick 7 min read Last updated June 23, 2023 Contents What is Network Flow Monitoring? How to Use Flow Monitoring and Packet Sampling Benefits of Flow Monitoring Top 3 Network Flow Monitoring Tools Network Flow Tools Compared Considerations for Flow Monitoring Is This Solution Right for You? Modern networking equipment is capable of processing billions of packets every second, but most of that work happens behind the scenes. Network Flow Monitoring, also known as packet sampling, aims to give network engineers some visibility into the vast amounts of traffic that cross our wired and wireless connections every day. In this blog post, we'll explore what network flow monitoring is and how it's used to keep traffic flowing smoothly and securely. We'll also look at three of the most popular flow monitoring technologies. Discover your weak points and strengthen your resilience: Run a Free Ransomware Readiness Test First name* Last name* Business Email* Please note that we will use your contact details to provide you with marketing communications from Varonis that we think may be of interest to you. You can unsubscribe from these communications at any time by clicking a link at the bottom of each email. For more information on our privacy practices, and how we're committed to protecting your information, please see our privacy policy. Get Your Assessment What is Network Flow Monitoring? × Network Flow Monitoring is the collection, analysis, and monitoring of traffic traversing a given network or network segment. The objectives may vary from troubleshooting connectivity issues to planning future bandwidth allocation. Flow monitoring and packet sampling can even be useful in identifying and remediating security issues. Flow monitoring gives networking teams a good idea of how a network is operating, providing insights into overall utilization, application usage, potential bottlenecks, anomalies that may signal security threats, and more. There are several different standards and formats used in network flow monitoring, including NetFlow, sFlow, and Internet Protocol Flow Information Export (IPFIX). Each works in a slightly different way, but all are distinct from port mirroring and deep packet inspection in that they do not capture the contents of every packet passing over a port or through a switch. However, flow monitoring does provide more information than SNMP, which is generally limited to broad statistics like overall packet and bandwidth use. How to Use Flow Monitoring and Packet Sampling Flow Monitoring and Packet Sampling can provide a number of benefits but do require careful planning and setup to be effective. Here are a couple of general tips for getting the most out of a flow monitoring solution: Clearly Define Your Use Case Different flow monitoring technologies – and even different versions of the same technology – have differences in both their capabilities and operation. NetFlow version 5, for example, doesn't support IPv4 or VLANs, making it of limited use in a highly segmented IPv6 network. You should have clear requirements and expectations documented before you even begin to set up flow monitoring on your network. Start with general questions like 'Where do we need visibility?', 'What specific types of information are we interested in?', and 'Do we need complete flows or is sampled data sufficient?'. More technical questions like 'Do we need visibility into both ingress and egress traffic?' and 'Which packet headers do we need to capture?' will help you narrow down your requirements. Cost is also a consideration, as is the potential performance of enabling flow monitoring on your network hardware. Nearly all modern networking equipment supports the capture and export of traffic flows, but collecting, analyzing, and monitoring those flows generally takes place in a third-party tool. These tools range widely in cost, with both open source and commercial options available. Know Your Hardware Although most enterprise-grade networking equipment supports some form of flow monitoring, the exact technology and capabilities vary, as does the configuration procedure. With your use case in mind, you'll want to evaluate what your hardware supports. In cases where existing routers/switches/firewalls don't support the features you need, standalone probes can also be used to capture flow monitoring data. Choose What to Monitor Before hopping onto the CLI of your closest router to turn the feature on, you'll need to decide exactly what type of network traffic flows you're interested in. This is usually defined in terms of a “tuple”. The most common version of NetFlow, for example, uses a “5-tuple” consisting of source and destination address, source and destination port, and the protocol field. Other flow monitoring technologies may use a “7-tuple” or even a “9-tuple”. Regardless, you'll need to do a little homework beforehand to ensure you're capturing the right traffic. Configure Your Flow Exporter Configuration of a flow monitoring technology is unfortunately not a uniform process, even between different products from the same vendor. You'll want to consult the documentation for not only your specific product but also the software version in use in your network. Configure Your Flow Collector and Analyzer(s) Exporting flow records is just the beginning. To get real value out of flow monitoring, you'll want to store your flow records in a collector and use an analyzer to make real sense of the data. A Flow Analyzer can help spot both short and long-term trends, visualize important data, and reveal new insights that you might have missed. Benefits of Flow Monitoring × Network Flow Monitoring can provide a number of benefits to organizations that implement it. Understanding how, when, and by whom a network is used can lead to greater resiliency, a lower cost of operation, and a faster time to resolution when things do go wrong. Here are just a few of the benefits that network flow monitoring can provide: More Efficient Use of Resources Imagine a highly congested network link or WAN interface. If relying on older monitoring techniques like SNMP, it may appear that additional bandwidth must be purchased. Flow Monitoring could give the organization deeper visibility into what and who is consuming all of the existing bandwidth. Perhaps an employee has set up an unauthorized file server, or a database is misconfigured. Remediating these types of issues would remove the requirement for more bandwidth, thus saving the organization money. When it does come time to upgrade network capacity, flow monitoring can provide a highly accurate basis from which to plan. Looking at historical data can yield a measure of how quickly traffic is increasing, giving business decision-makers an idea of how soon upgrades may be required. Detecting Anomalous Traffic and other Network Security Threats The ability to detect anomalous network traffic can be a major incentive to implementing a flow monitoring solution. Monitoring traffic within a network, as opposed to at the boundary or perimeter, can help identify threats that have slipped past other types of defenses. Large “east-west” traffic spikes between machines in the network could be indicative of a worm or virus propagating. A spike in outbound traffic could signal data exfiltration or command and control messages. A large amount of incoming traffic might be the first wave of a DDoS attack. Flow Monitoring is a great complement to signature-based security technologies like Intrusion Detection Systems and Antivirus. New threats might not match any existing signatures, but with flow monitoring, a deviation from standard network behavior can still raise alarms. These types of solutions can also be used to narrow down the focus of a threat hunting campaign by providing security analysts a good starting point to do a deeper packet capture with a tool like Wireshark. Verifying Application Performance and Quality of Service (QoS) Since flow monitoring can be enabled for a particular protocol, it can be immensely useful in verifying or troubleshooting the performance of business-critical applications like VoIP or videoconferencing. Network administrators can see the path(s) these types of important packets take through the network, and can also ensure the correct Class of Service is being applied to the traffic. Flow monitoring is also useful in measuring the impact of a new application, network configuration, or change in the number of users accessing the network. Top 3 Network Flow Monitoring Tools × There are a variety of flow monitoring solutions available on the market today, but the top three are Netflow, sFlow, and IPFIX. Here's a brief look at each of these top options: What is NetFlow? NetFlow is the original flow monitoring solution, originally developed by Cisco in the late 1990s. Several different versions exist, but most deployments are based on either NetFlow v5 or NetFlow v9. While each version has different capabilities, the basic operation remains the same: First, a router, switch, firewall, or another type of device will capture information on the network “flows” – basically a set of packets that share a common set of characteristics like source and destination address, source, and destination port, and protocol type. After a flow has gone dormant or a predefined amount of time has passed, the device will export the flow records to an entity known as a “flow collector”. Finally, a “flow analyzer” makes sense of those records, providing insights in the form of visualizations, statistics, and detailed historical and real-time reporting. In practice, collectors and analyzers are often a single entity, often combined into a larger network performance monitoring solution. NetFlow operates on a stateful basis. When a client machine reaches out to a server, NetFlow will begin capturing and aggregating metadata from the flow. After the session is terminated, NetFlow will export a single complete record to the collector. Though it's still commonly used, NetFlow v5 has a number of limitations. The fields exported are fixed, monitoring is supported only in the ingress direction, and modern technologies like IPv6, MPLS, and VXLAN aren't supported. NetFlow v9, also branded as Flexible NetFlow (FNF), addresses some of these limitations, allowing users to build custom templates and adding support for newer technologies. Many vendors also have their own proprietary implementations of NetFlow, such as jFlow from Juniper and NetStream from Huawei. Though the configuration may differ somewhat, these implementations often produce flow records that are compatible with NetFlow collectors and analyzers. What is sFlow? Short for sampled flow, sFlow takes a slightly different approach to network monitoring than other options. Where NetFlow statefully tracks flows, sFlow works by randomly sampling the full packet headers of a given flow at a predetermined interval. This can cut down on bandwidth and CPU utilization on the switch or router capturing flow information, but may also reduce the accuracy of the information collected. sFlow does, however, capture deeper levels of information than NetFlow, including full packet headers and even partial packet payloads. sFlow is supported on a wide range of networking equipment, even on many products from Cisco. On the collector/analyzer side, sFlow exports records that are incompatible with NetFlow, but many network monitoring and analysis tools support both formats. sFlow exports sampled packets in near real-time, and unlike NetFlow, there is no flow cache on the network device. This can make sFlow a more scalable option in very high-speed networks. However, it's critical to configure an appropriate sampling rate. Sampling a greater percentage of the total packets can yield greater accuracy, but too high a percentage negates the benefits of statistical sampling. There is, unfortunately, no “correct” sampling rate; the number of variables involved means that each network will be different. What is IPFIX? An IETF standard that emerged in the early 2000s, Internet Protocol Flow Information Export (IPFIX) is extremely similar to NetFlow. In fact, NetFlow v9 served as the basis for IPFIX. The primary difference between the two is that IPFIX is an open standard, and is supported by many networking vendors apart from Cisco. With the exception of a few additional fields added in IPFIX, the formats are otherwise nearly identical. In fact, IPFIX is sometimes even referred to as “NetFlow v10”. Owing in part to its similarities to NetFlow, IPFIX enjoys wide support among network monitoring solutions as well as network equipment. Network Flow Tools Compared Considerations for Flow Monitoring As discussed previously, there are some important considerations when deploying any flow monitoring solution, whether it be NetFlow, sFlow, or IPFIX. The choice of what platform or tool you use to analyze your flow records can greatly impact the value you get out of flow monitoring. You'll also want to consider storage requirements, which can vary based on how much traffic you're monitoring or sampling. Perhaps most importantly, you'll want to consider whether flow monitoring is even the most appropriate type of Network Traffic Analysis for your needs. Do you need the deeper level of inspection possible with a full packet capture? Or will metadata and summarized information suffice? Is This Solution Right for You? Flow Monitoring is an indispensable addition to any network engineer's toolbox, but it's not the be-all and end-all, especially when it comes to security. Packet sampling in particular can miss highly sophisticated threats that don't make a lot of noise. Pairing the network-centric perspectives of flow monitoring with a data-centric solution like the Varonis Data Protection Platform can help you avoid blind spots, identify advanced threats, and maintain compliance. If you're interested in learning more about how Varonis can complement your existing monitoring solutions, schedule a one-on-one demo today! What should I do now?
